#Gradient Descent

Hi

Introduction¶

Gradient descent is an optimization aglorithm that is used for one thing: finding the local minimum of the differentiable function. How we are able to find that is once we find a spot of the function’s gradient, we need to repeat the steps until we eventually get to the functions local minumum.

This algorithm was first introduced to the world by Augustin-Louis Cauchy back in the year of 1847. He created this algorithm to help solve larger optimization problems in a short amount of time that were used in studying astrology. Although it’s still used in astrology today, we also use it in our daily live’s without realizing that we are using gradient descent.

For example, when we are going to class we need to find the quickest way to class. Also when we are driving, we are wanting to get to our desination the quickest so we can save our time and gas. When it comes to making huge purchases in life, like a vehicle. Once we know what vehicle that we want to purchase, we usually travel to different dealerships to find that vehicle with the cheapest price.

Theory¶

While gradient descent is the main section that we will be talking about, there are also other types of this algorithm. There’s three types of algorithms: batch, socratic, and mini-batch gradient descent. While these are all of the gradient descent algorithm category, they are used for different problems.

  1. Batch Gradient Descent - It calculates the error of each example in the dataset but only after all of the training values have been evaluated and the model has been upgraded. This type is great for producing a stable error and convergience.

  2. Socratic Gradient Descent - This is used within the dataset. In other words, it updates the parameters for each example one-by-one. Depending on the problem, the Socratic Gradient Descent can be quicker than the batch.

  3. Mini-Batch Gradient Descent -This version is a mixture of both the batch and socratic gradient descent. It’s the go-to method because it simply splits the training dataset into small batches and preforms a update for every batch. This type is the most common type of gradient descent.

When it comes to to finding the gradient descent, we need to understand what we are trying to find. When you try to calculate the gradient descent, you need to follow the negative gradient of the objective function in order to locate the minimum of a function. The minimum of a function is also called the cost.

When it’s postive slope then its a minimum, when it’s negative, then its a maximum.

Examples¶

Example 1 worked out

REAL LIFE EXAMPLE

Gradient Descent basically means finding the local minimum the function.

Gradient Descent is also called steepest descent which is a tool to find a local minimum or local maximum of a point.If we are on the top of the Mt. Everest and looking for a pathway to come to the base. In this is case, what we need to do is predict the way which would be shorter as well as less risky to us.

Here we need to find the connecting points from top of the Mt. Everest to the base camp. For this, we simply create the parabola.

Example 2¶

Let’s say that we are trying to find someone peoples weight and height. Here are the following x is the weight and y is the height.

1.(0.5 , 1.4)

2.(2.3 , 1.9)

3.(2.9 ,3.2)

With this algorithm we are able to predict their height with a simple equation:

Predicted Height = intercept + slope x weight

We can start to solve the problem by doing the following:

  1. Start by using GD to find the intercept.

  2. Then we will use GD to solve the Intercept and the Slope.

  3. We plug can plug any number in the Least Squares section. We chose 0.64.

The updated problem will now look like this:

Predicted Height = Intercept + 0.64 * Weight

To find the intercept we can plug any number in this area, for this example, let’s choose 0:

Predicted Height = 0 + 0.64 * Weight

We do this so the gradient descent will have something to improve on. It will also help create a line from the example above (its like y=mx+b).

In this example, we will evaluate how well this line fits the data, known as the Loss Function.

Since we are looking at the first coordinate, we plug the x value (which is weight) into its spot and find the predicted height. Then the problem will look like this:

**Predicted Height = 0 + 0.64 * 0.5

Once we got the Predicted Height, we need to calculate the difference between the Observed and Predicted Height. The Observed Height is the y value of the coordinates of the points from above.

1.4 - 0.32 -> 1.1

Sum of the squared residuals = 1.1^2 + 0.4^2 + 1.3^2 = 3.1

We need to find the lowest value but with GD, the closer that you get to the optimal value, the steps get closer. the farther, the bigger steps that GD will do.

Looking back at the problem that we were supposed to be solving, we will need to continue to solve the Sum of the Squared Residuals

NOTE: We can now plug in any value for the Intercept and get a new Predicted Height

For the next person it’ll be:

(1.9 - (intercept + 0.64 x 2.3))^2

The last persons equation will be:

(3.2 - (intercept + 0.64 x 2.9))^2

We now have an equation for the curve and we will need to find the derivative of the function.

Now we need to find the derivative of the sum of the squared residuals = (d/d intercept) Sum of squared residuals

To do this we apply the chain rule and get the following:

-2(1.4-Intercept+0.64x0.5)+-2(1.9-Intercept+0.64x2.3))+-2(3.2-Intercept+0.64x2.9))

Now gradient descent will use it to find where the Sum of the Squared Residuals is the lowest.

Since we have the step size, we can calculate a new intercept:

NEW INTERCEPT = OLD INTERCEPT - STEP SIZE

To see how much the residuals shrink when the intercept = 0.57 we just need to plug the 0.57 into the new intercept.

-2(1.4-(0.57+0.64x0.5) + -2(1.9-(0.57+0.64x0.5))+-2(3.2-(0.57+0.64x2.9)) -> -2.3

STEP SIZE = -2.3 x LEARNING RATE STEP SIZE = -2.3 x 0.1 -> -0.23 NEW INTERCEPT = 0.57 - (-0.23) -> 0.8 NEW INTERCEPT

Now we need to go back to the derivative equation from earlier and plug in the 0.8 now -2(1.4-(0.8+0.64x0.5) + -2(1.9-(0.8+0.64x0.5))+-2(3.2-(0.8+0.64x2.9)) -> -0.9

the new step size is now -0.9 * 0.1 = -0.09 new intercept is 0.89 when we do it again, the new intercept is 0.92

When we do this 6 times, the GD for the intercept is 0.95. NOTE: The Least Squares estimate is also 0.95

GD STOPS WHEN THE STEP SIZE IS VERY CLOSE TO 0 STEP SIZE = SLOPE * LEARNING RATE

Example 3¶

Find the gradient descent of \(y=(x+5)^2\)

def f(x):
  return (x+5)**2

f(5)

def derf(x):
  return 2*(x+5)

initialize = 3
weight = 0.12
x = initialize

update = x - weight*derf(x)
update

  return x- weight*derf(x)

update = graddescent(update,weight)
print(update)

##Example 4

Find the local minimum of \(f(x) = (x-3)^4\)

import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import numpy as np

def f(x):
    return (x-2)**3
print(f(5))

def derf(x):
    return 4*(x-3)
i=5
w=0.05 
x=i

def gradient_descent(x,weight):
    return x-weight*derf(x)

X=np.arange(2,10,0.4)
Y=[f(i) for i in X]

X1=[]
y1=[]

for i in range(5):
    X1.append(x)
    y1.append(f(x))
    x=gradient_descent(x,w)
   
plt.plot(X,Y)
plt.scatter(X1,y1,marker="+",color="black")
plt.show()

27

  1. Find the gradient descent of \(y=(x+3)^4\)

  2. Find the gradient descent of \(y=(x+6)^5\)

  3. Find the gradient descent of the function: \(y= (2x+4)^2\)

  4. The function is \(y=(9x+2)^2\), what is the gradient descent?

  5. Find the gradient descent of \(y=(12x+2)^3\)

  6. The function is \(y=(x+2)^2\), find the gradient descent

  7. Find the gradient descent of \(y=(3x-4)^2\)

  8. What is the gradient descent of \(y=(5x-4)^4\)

  9. Find the gradient descent of \(y=(x-5)^2\)

  10. The function is \(y= (4x-6)^4\), what is the gradient descent?
